For simple, one-to-many distributions like broadcasting ARI updates, a purely **event-driven (choreographed)** pattern is effective: a single `ARIUpdated` event is published, and multiple independent services (egress adapters) react to it. However, for complex, multi-step, and long-running processes like the reservation lifecycle, an **orchestration** pattern provides better control, visibility, and error handling. This pattern is crucial for maintaining data consistency across systems and handling failures gracefully in processes that span multiple services and time."

The process begins with Change Data Capture (CDC) from the channel manager's primary database (e.g., Postgres), which serves as the source of truth. Any modification to ARI data triggers an event. This can be implemented using database triggers or logical decoding (e.g., WAL2JSON for Postgres). Each worker, or group of workers, is responsible for a specific external platform (e.g., Booking.com, Expedia). The worker uses a corresponding 'egress adapter' to translate the ARI update from the canonical data model into the platform-specific format and protocol (e.g., REST/JSON, OTA XML).       "reservation_lifecycle_management": "Reservation lifecycle management is handled by a finite state machine to robustly track and manage the status of each booking. A reservation transitions through various states, such as 'Pending', 'Confirmed', 'Modified', 'Cancelled', 'Checked-In', 'Checked-Out', and 'No-Show'. Each transition is triggered by a specific event, for example, a 'BookingRequestReceived' event from an OTA webhook moves a new reservation to 'Pending'. A successful confirmation from the property moves it to 'Confirmed'. This state machine is critical for ensuring process integrity. It is coupled with compensation actions (part of the SAGA pattern) to handle failures. For instance, if a booking is confirmed on the OTA side but the subsequent inventory update fails, a compensation action is triggered to revert the booking confirmation or flag it for manual intervention, preventing data inconsistencies and potential overbookings. This entire workflow can be orchestrated using Celery chains or a dedicated workflow engine like Temporal.",
      "reconciliation_methods": "To ensure data consistency between the channel manager and over 25 external platforms, a multi-pronged reconciliation strategy is employed. The primary methods include: 1) **Periodic Diffing:** Scheduled Celery tasks run periodically to pull ARI and reservation data from each external platform. This data is then compared ('diffed') against the channel manager's internal state (the canonical model). Any discrepancies found trigger corrective updates to be sent, ensuring the systems converge. 2) **Versioning and Sequence Numbers:** Each ARI update sent to a provider can include a version number or a sequence number. The provider can acknowledge the last version received. This allows the channel manager to detect and resend missed or out-of-order updates, providing a more granular check than periodic diffing. 3) **Ledger-Based Reconciliation:** For critical data like inventory, a detailed, immutable ledger of all changes (e.g., new booking, cancellation, manual block) is maintained. This provides a complete audit trail and allows for the exact state of inventory at any point in time to be reconstructed, which is invaluable for resolving complex discrepancies.",
      "conflict_resolution_logic": "Given the high volume of updates from multiple sources (hotel PMS, various OTAs), a clear conflict resolution strategy is essential to prevent data corruption. The primary strategies considered are: 1) **Last-Write-Wins (LWW):** This is the simplest approach, where the most recent update, based on its timestamp, overwrites any previous data. While easy to implement, it is risky as it can lead to lost updates if clock skew between systems is not managed. Clock skew can be mitigated by using NTP for time synchronization. 2) **Business-Rule-Based Logic:** A more sophisticated approach involves defining specific rules to resolve conflicts. For example, a rule might state that updates originating from the hotel's own Property Management System (PMS) always have the highest priority for availability changes, while a modification to a reservation from the originating OTA takes precedence over other changes. 3) **Vector Clocks:** For maximum data integrity in a highly distributed environment, vector clocks can be implemented. This technique tracks the causal history of updates, allowing the system to detect concurrent, conflicting changes and apply a more intelligent resolution logic or flag the conflict for manual review, rather than blindly overwriting data."

    "reliability_and_scalability_patterns": {
      "rate_limiting_strategy": "The system will employ an adaptive, client-side rate limiting strategy to respect the diverse and strict API limits of each provider, such as Booking.com's 10,000 calls/minute general limit. Acknowledging that Celery's built-in per-worker rate limiter is insufficient for global control, a more sophisticated database-backed task dispatcher will be implemented. This involves logging all outgoing API tasks to a database model with their target provider and priority. A scheduled Celery task will then act as a dispatcher, periodically querying this table. It will fetch a batch of tasks for each provider that is within its specific rate limit (e.g., using a token bucket algorithm stored in Redis or the database) and then enqueue them into Celery for immediate execution. This approach provides centralized control over API usage, prevents worker starvation from failed rate limit checks, allows for task prioritization (e.g., new bookings over bulk ARI updates), and ensures the system gracefully handles `429 Too Many Requests` errors by simply delaying dispatch rather than re-queueing tasks.",
      "idempotency_implementation": "To ensure safe retries of non-idempotent operations, particularly `POST` requests for creating new reservations or `PUT` requests for updates, the system will implement idempotency keys. For every such operation, the client (a Celery worker) will generate a unique idempotency key (e.g., a UUID). This key will be sent in the HTTP header of the API request (e.g., `Idempotency-Key: <unique-uuid>`). The receiving server (the external OTA/GDS) is expected to store this key for a limited deduplication window (e.g., 24 hours). If a request with the same key is received within this window, the server will not re-process the request but will instead return the original response. This pattern is critical for preventing duplicate bookings or conflicting updates that could arise from network failures and subsequent automatic retries, thus achieving an 'exactly-once-like' processing outcome for critical operations.",
      "retry_and_backoff_policy": "All interactions with external APIs will be wrapped in a robust retry policy to handle transient failures like network issues, timeouts, or temporary server errors (5xx status codes). The strategy will be **exponential backoff with jitter**. When a task fails, instead of retrying immediately, it will be rescheduled with a delay that increases exponentially with each subsequent failure (e.g., 1s, 2s, 4s, 8s...). A random 'jitter' (a small random time offset) will be added to this delay. This prevents the 'thundering herd' problem, where many clients, recovering from a widespread outage, retry simultaneously and overload the recovering service. This logic will be implemented within the Celery task definition using `self.retry(countdown=...)`. A maximum number of retries will be defined to prevent infinite loops, after which the task will be considered permanently failed and routed for special handling.",
      "failure_handling_and_dlqs": "To handle persistent failures and 'poison messages' (messages that consistently cause a consumer to crash), the system will utilize Dead Letter Queues (DLQs). In RabbitMQ, this is configured via a Dead Letter Exchange (DLX). After a message fails processing a predefined number of times (as per the retry policy), it will be automatically rejected and routed by the broker to a designated DLQ. In Kafka, a similar pattern can be implemented by catching the exception in the consumer and explicitly publishing the problematic message to a separate `.dlq` topic. These DLQs will not have active consumers. Instead, they will serve as a holding area for failed messages, triggering alerts for the operations team. This allows for manual inspection, analysis of the failure cause, and potential manual correction and replay of the message, all without blocking the processing of valid messages in the main queue."
    },

      "django_model_design": "The Django model structure will be built around a comprehensive Canonical Data Model (CDM) to normalize data from all integrated OTAs, GDS, and metasearch platforms. This approach is critical for maintaining a single source of truth and simplifying business logic. The core of the design involves creating Django models for each canonical entity identified in the research, such as `Property`, `RoomType`, `RatePlan`, `Inventory`, `Restrictions`, `Policy`, `Promotion`, `Reservation`, and `Payment`. \n\nKey design principles for the models include:\n\n1.  **Core Entities and Fields:**\n    *   `Property`: Will store static property information like name, address, timezone ('Asia/Kolkata'), currency ('INR'), and GST details (`gstin`, `legal_name`).\n    *   `RoomType`: Defines the physical rooms, including `name`, `max_occupancy`, `base_occupancy`, and `amenities`.\n    *   `RatePlan`: Represents different pricing strategies, linking to a `RoomType`. It will include fields for `name`, `meal_plan` (Enum: 'ROOM_ONLY', 'BREAKFAST'), `inclusions`, and foreign keys to `Policy` models.\n    *   `Inventory`: A time-series model tracking `available_rooms` for a specific `RoomType` on a given `date`.\n    *   `Reservation`: Captures all booking details, including `provider_id`, `provider_reservation_id`, `status` (Enum: 'CONFIRMED', 'CANCELLED'), guest details, and pricing components (`base_room_rate`, `total_taxes_fees`).\n    *   `Policy`: A flexible model to define `CANCELLATION`, `PREPAYMENT`, and `NO_SHOW` rules, using a JSONB field (`details`) to store complex, provider-specific rule structures.\n    *   `TaxFee`: A model to handle various taxes and fees, crucial for the Indian market. It will include fields for `type` (PERCENTAGE, FLAT), `value`, `is_inclusive`, and a `gst_component` (Enum: 'CGST', 'SGST', 'IGST') to correctly handle GST calculations.\n\n2.  **Extensibility with JSONB:** To ensure future-proofing and prevent data loss from non-standard provider fields, each major model (`Property`, `RoomType`, `RatePlan`, `Reservation`) will include a `provider_specific_data` field of type `JSONB` (using `django.db.models.JSONField`). This field will act as a flexible container to store any unique attributes or raw data from a provider's API that does not map directly to the canonical schema. This is vital for extensibility and backward-compatibility as provider APIs evolve.\n\n3.  **Mapping Strategy:** A critical component is the mapping between the internal canonical model and each external platform's specific identifiers. This will be implemented by adding a `provider_mappings` JSONB field to entities like `Property`, `RoomType`, and `RatePlan`. This field will store a dictionary mapping a provider's name (e.g., 'booking.com') to its unique ID for that entity (e.g., `{'booking.com': '12345', 'expedia': '67890'}`). This avoids the need for numerous separate mapping tables and keeps the mapping information co-located with the entity itself.\n\n4.  **Handling Complex Pricing:** The models will support complex pricing rules such as occupancy-based pricing and Length of Stay (LOS) pricing. This will be achieved through fields like `base_occupancy`, `extra_adult_charge` on the `RatePlan` model, and potentially a `los_based_rates` JSONB field to store LOS-specific rate modifiers. Derived rates (e.g., a non-refundable rate that is 10% off a flexible rate) will be handled with `is_derived`, `parent_rate_plan_id`, and `derivation_rule` fields on the `RatePlan` model.",
      "celery_task_design": "The design of Celery tasks will be function-specific, incorporating robust patterns for reliability, rate-limiting, and idempotency to manage interactions with over 25 external APIs.\n\n1.  **Polling Tasks:** For platforms that do not provide webhooks for reservation updates, `Celery Beat` will schedule periodic polling tasks. For example, a task could poll Booking.com's `/reservationssummary` endpoint. These tasks will be designed with adaptive polling logic, potentially increasing frequency for properties with high booking velocity and decreasing it for others to use resources efficiently. Each task will process a batch of properties to minimize overhead.\n\n2.  **Webhook Handling Tasks:** For real-time updates, dedicated API endpoints in the Django app will receive webhook notifications (e.g., for new bookings, modifications, or cancellations from Expedia or Tripadvisor). These endpoints will perform minimal validation and immediately enqueue a Celery task with the payload. The task will then handle the actual business logic of creating or updating a reservation in the database. This decouples the webhook ingestion from processing, ensuring the endpoint can respond quickly and reliably. These tasks must be idempotent to handle potential duplicate webhook deliveries from providers.\n\n3.  **Batch ARI (Availability, Rates, Inventory) Update Tasks:** To manage the high volume of ARI updates and respect API rate limits, updates will be batched and coalesced. When a hotel's inventory changes, instead of an immediate API call, the change will be logged to a database model or a Redis queue. A scheduled Celery task will then run every few seconds, gather all pending changes for a specific provider, coalesce multiple updates for the same room/date into the single latest state, and send them as a single batch request if the provider's API supports it (e.g., `OTA_HotelInvNotif`).\n\n4.  **Reconciliation Jobs:** To ensure data consistency and prevent overbookings, `Celery Beat` will schedule regular reconciliation tasks. These long-running tasks will, for a given property, pull the current ARI and reservation list from a provider's API, compare it ('diff') against the state in the canonical database, and generate corrective tasks to resolve any discrepancies. This is a critical fallback to catch any missed updates from webhooks or polling.\n\n5.  **Reliability and Rate-Limiting Patterns:**\n    *   **Idempotency:** All tasks that modify state (e.g., creating a reservation, updating inventory) will be designed to be idempotent. This will be achieved by using a unique identifier from the source event (e.g., `provider_reservation_id`) to check if the operation has already been performed.\n    *   **Retries with Exponential Backoff:** Tasks will use Celery's built-in retry mechanism (`self.retry(exc=e, countdown=...)`) with exponential backoff and jitter to handle transient API errors or rate-limiting responses (e.g., HTTP 429). This prevents overwhelming a struggling external service.\n    *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** To manage strict provider rate limits (e.g., Booking.com's 10,000 calls/minute), a central database-based task dispatcher will be implemented. Instead of tasks calling APIs directly, they will be logged to a 'Task Dispatch' model. A single, scheduled Celery task will then query this model, pull a number of tasks that fits within the rate limit for each provider, and dispatch them to workers. This provides global, cross-worker rate limit enforcement.\n    *   **Dead Letter Queues (DLQs):** Messages that consistently fail after multiple retries (poison messages) will be routed to a DLQ in RabbitMQ/Kafka for manual inspection, preventing them from blocking the main processing queues.",
      "message_queue_selection": "The choice between RabbitMQ and Kafka as the message broker for Celery depends on the system's target scale, architectural philosophy, and specific functional requirements. Both are viable, but they serve different primary purposes.\n\n**RabbitMQ:**\n*   **Rationale:** RabbitMQ is a feature-rich, traditional message broker that excels at complex routing and task management. It would be the preferred choice if the architecture prioritizes a smart broker/dumb consumer model, where the broker handles sophisticated routing logic. Its support for the AMQP protocol allows for flexible patterns like topic, direct, and fanout exchanges, which are useful for routing different types of tasks (e.g., sending high-priority booking notifications to a dedicated queue while routing low-priority report generation to another).\n*   **Trade-offs:** While highly reliable for task queues, RabbitMQ is not designed as a durable event log. Message replay is not a built-in feature, and its throughput, while high, may be a limiting factor in extremely large-scale, event-streaming scenarios compared to Kafka.\n*   **Best Fit:** For a system focused on reliable, asynchronous task execution with complex routing needs and per-message acknowledgements, RabbitMQ is an excellent and often simpler choice to set up and manage.\n\n**Kafka:**\n*   **Rationale:** Kafka is fundamentally a distributed, persistent event log, designed for extremely high-throughput event streaming. It would be the superior choice if the system is designed as a true event-driven architecture, where data changes (events) are the central artifact. An 'ARIUpdated' event, for example, could be published to a Kafka topic and consumed independently by multiple services: one to update OTAs, another for internal analytics, and a third for logging. Its durability allows for message replay, which is invaluable for recovery, debugging, and re-processing historical data with new logic.\n*   **Trade-offs:** Kafka is more complex to set up and operate than RabbitMQ. It provides fewer out-of-the-box routing features, as the logic often resides in the consumers. While Celery can use Kafka as a broker, it doesn't leverage all of Kafka's streaming capabilities natively, and a more tailored consumer implementation might be needed to fully exploit its power.\n*   **Best Fit:** For a large-scale platform aiming for a decoupled, microservices-based architecture where business events need to be reliably captured and processed by multiple downstream systems, Kafka provides the scalability and durability required.\n\n**Recommendation:** A hybrid approach could be optimal. Use Kafka as the central event bus for critical, high-volume data like ARI changes and reservation events, ensuring durability and replayability. Use RabbitMQ for more traditional, transient background tasks and complex routing scenarios where the durability of an event log is not required."

    "india_specific_requirements_and_compliance": {
      "gst_handling_strategy": "The strategy for handling India's Goods and Services Tax (GST) is built into the canonical data model and business logic. The 'Tax/Fee' entity includes a 'gst_component' field to specify whether a tax is Central GST (CGST), State GST (SGST), or Integrated GST (IGST). The logic for applying the correct components is based on the 'place of supply' rules. If the property's location (state) and the guest's location (state) are the same (intra-state transaction), CGST and SGST are calculated and applied. If they are in different states (inter-state transaction), IGST is applied. The system must also account for the role of the Online Travel Agency (OTA) as an Electronic Commerce Operator (ECO) under Indian GST law. The channel manager needs to determine if the OTA is responsible for collecting and remitting the tax (under sections like 9(5) or 52 of the CGST Act) or if the liability remains with the hotel property, as this significantly impacts the data flow and invoicing responsibilities.",
      "invoicing_data_requirements": "To generate GST-compliant invoices in India, the system must capture and store a specific set of mandatory data fields. These fields are incorporated into the 'Property' and 'Reservation' entities of the canonical data model. The required fields are: 1) **Supplier Details:** The hotel's legal name, full address, and GST Identification Number (GSTIN). 2) **Recipient Details:** The guest's name and full address. If it is a business-to-business (B2B) transaction, the recipient's GSTIN is also mandatory. 3) **Invoice Details:** A unique invoice number and the invoice date. 4) **Service Details:** The Harmonized System of Nomenclature / Service Accounting Code (HSN/SAC) for the accommodation service. 5) **Financial Breakdown:** The taxable value of the service (rate before tax), the applicable GST rates, and the specific amounts for CGST, SGST, and IGST. 6) **Total Amount:** The final invoice total including all taxes. 7) **Place of Supply:** The state where the service is supplied, which is critical for determining the correct GST components.",
      "local_payment_method_support": "The strategy for supporting local Indian payment methods is to ensure the system's data model and payment processing logic are flexible enough to accommodate them. The canonical 'Payment' entity is designed with a 'payment_method' enum field that explicitly includes 'UPI' (Unified Payments Interface) alongside standard methods like 'CREDIT_CARD', 'BANK_TRANSFER', and 'CASH'. This ensures that when a reservation's payment is processed via UPI, either through an OTA or a direct booking engine connected to the channel manager, the transaction can be correctly recorded and tracked. The actual integration would involve connecting with a payment gateway provider that supports UPI transactions. The channel manager's role is to pass the necessary transaction details to the gateway and correctly handle the success or failure responses to update the reservation's 'payment_status' in the canonical model."
    },

    "fallback_and_alternative_connectivity_strategies": {
      "aggregator_integration_plan": "The strategy for connecting to OTAs via third-party aggregators is essential for achieving comprehensive market coverage, especially given that direct API access for many India-focused OTAs (e.g., Ixigo, Via.com, Paytm Travel) and restricted suppliers (e.g., Airbnb, HRS) is either unavailable, not publicly documented, or commercially restrictive. The plan involves a multi-layered approach:\n\n1.  **Prioritize Direct Integration for Key Players:** Focus initial development resources on building direct API integrations with major global OTAs that provide robust, well-documented partner APIs, such as Booking.com, Expedia Group, and Agoda. This ensures a high-quality connection to the largest sources of demand.\n\n2.  **Integrate with a Primary Indian Aggregator:** For the long tail of Indian OTAs, the strategy is to integrate with a major B2B travel platform that already has extensive connections. The research identifies **TBO (Travel Boutique Online)** as a key target. By integrating with TBO's API, the channel manager can gain access to a wide range of Indian travel providers through a single connection, significantly reducing development effort and time-to-market.\n\n3.  **Leverage Global Switches and Connectivity Providers:** To connect with a broader range of global distributors, including corporate travel specialists like HRS and other hard-to-reach channels, the plan includes integrating with a global connectivity switch. The research points to **RateGain** (an Indian company that acquired DHISCO) and **eRevMax (RateTiger)** as powerful options. These platforms specialize in distribution and have pre-existing certified connections to hundreds of channels, including GDS, OTAs, and wholesalers.\n\n4.  **Utilize Specialized Integrators for Specific Platforms:** For certain platforms like MakeMyTrip or HRS, where direct API documentation is scarce, the plan is to engage with third-party integration specialists identified in the research, such as **FlightsLogic** or **Trawex Technologies**. These companies offer pre-built 'Activity XML Out' or similar solutions that can be integrated into the Django application, serving as a dedicated bridge to that specific platform.\n\n5.  **Airbnb Workaround:** Since Airbnb's API access is currently restricted, the primary strategy is to guide hotel clients to use an existing Airbnb Preferred Software Partner (like Guesty or SiteMinder) that already has an established connection. The channel manager could then potentially integrate with that partner's API (e.g., Guesty's JSON API) as a secondary, indirect path to manage the Airbnb channel.",
      "bedbank_integration_plan": "The plan for leveraging connections to major bedbanks is a cornerstone of the connectivity strategy, designed to rapidly expand inventory access and provide a robust fallback distribution channel. The primary focus will be on integrating with **Hotelbeds**, which the research identifies as a dominant global bedbank, having consolidated other major wholesalers like Tourico Holidays and GTA.\n\n1.  **Direct API Integration with Hotelbeds:** The core of the plan is to build a direct, two-way integration with the Hotelbeds API suite. The research confirms that Hotelbeds provides a modern REST API with support for both JSON and XML, which is well-suited for a Django/Celery architecture. The integration will be broken down into three parts, corresponding to Hotelbeds' API groups:\n    *   **Hotel Content API:** A set of Celery tasks will be developed to periodically pull and cache static data from this API. This includes property details, room descriptions, amenities, images, and other rich content. This data will populate the channel manager's canonical database, reducing the need for real-time content calls during the booking process.\n    *   **Booking API:** This will be the most active integration point. Celery tasks will be designed to handle real-time availability checks, retrieve quotes, create new bookings, manage existing reservations (modifications/cancellations), and process confirmations. This integration is critical for the live transactional flow.\n    *   **Cache API:** For advanced use cases, the plan includes exploring the Cache API. This API is designed for handling large volumes of pricing and availability data, which can be used for building analytics, competitive intelligence features, or offering packaged travel deals.\n\n2.  **Strategic Value of the Bedbank Connection:** The Hotelbeds integration serves multiple strategic purposes:\n    *   **Inventory Aggregation:** It provides immediate access to a massive global inventory of hotels, which can be distributed to all connected OTAs and other sales channels.\n    *   **Alternative Connectivity Path:** Many hotels and smaller OTAs are already connected to Hotelbeds. This integration can therefore serve as an alternative or fallback path to manage a property's distribution if a direct connection to a specific OTA is not performing well or is unavailable.\n    *   **Rate Sourcing:** It allows the channel manager to source net rates from the bedbank, which can then be marked up and distributed, enabling more flexible and competitive pricing strategies."
    },
    "observability_and_monitoring_plan": {
      "logging_strategy": "A structured, contextual logging approach will be implemented across all components of the system, including the Django application, Celery workers, and any other services. Logs will be generated in a machine-readable format like JSON. Each log entry will contain not only the message but also a rich context, including a unique correlation ID (or trace ID) that is propagated through the entire request lifecycle. This allows for filtering and searching logs related to a single transaction as it moves across different services. Other contextual information will include the service name, task name, relevant business identifiers (e.g., `property_id`, `reservation_id`, `provider_name`), and performance timings. This structured approach facilitates easier parsing, searching, and analysis in a centralized logging platform (e.g., ELK Stack, Datadog, or Grafana Loki).",
      "key_metrics_to_track": "A comprehensive set of key metrics will be tracked to monitor the health, performance, and reliability of the channel manager. These will be exposed via a monitoring system like Prometheus and visualized in dashboards (e.g., Grafana). Critical metrics include: \n1. **API Interaction Metrics (per provider):** API call latency (average, p95, p99), error rates (categorized by HTTP status code, e.g., 4xx vs. 5xx), and rate limit consumption (requests per minute).\n2. **Message Queue Metrics:** Queue depth/size (number of messages waiting), message latency (time from publish to consume), and consumer lag (for Kafka).\n3. **Celery Task Metrics:** Number of tasks succeeded, failed, and retried (per task type), and task execution duration (average, p95, p99).\n4. **Business-Level Metrics:** End-to-end propagation time for ARI updates, number of bookings processed, number of synchronization discrepancies detected by reconciliation jobs, and price accuracy scores from metasearch platforms like Google Hotels.\n5. **System Metrics:** CPU and memory utilization of services, database connection pool usage, and response times of the Django web application.",
      "distributed_tracing_plan": "To gain deep visibility into request flows within the distributed architecture, distributed tracing will be implemented using a standard like OpenTelemetry. Each incoming request (e.g., a webhook notification or an API call from the UI) will be assigned a unique trace ID. This trace ID will be propagated across all subsequent service calls, message queue headers, and asynchronous Celery tasks related to that initial request. This allows for the reconstruction of the entire request journey, visualizing it as a flame graph. This is invaluable for debugging complex issues, identifying performance bottlenecks (e.g., which external API call is slowing down the entire process), and understanding the interactions between different microservices. Traces will be sent to a compatible backend like Jaeger, Zipkin, or a commercial observability platform for analysis.",
      "alerting_strategy": "A proactive alerting strategy will be established to notify the operations team of critical issues before they significantly impact users or revenue. Alerts will be configured based on thresholds set against the key metrics. The strategy will focus on actionable alerts to avoid alert fatigue. Key alert categories include:\n1. **Critical Failures:** A sudden spike in 5xx error rates from a major OTA's API, a high rate of Celery task failures for critical workflows (e.g., reservation processing), or a rapid increase in the size of a Dead Letter Queue (DLQ).\n2. **Performance Degradation:** A significant increase in API response latency or message queue processing time that exceeds defined Service Level Objectives (SLOs).\n3. **Saturation:** System resources like CPU or memory reaching critical levels, or message queues growing uncontrollably, indicating a processing bottleneck.\n4. **Business Logic Failures:** An alert triggered if the price accuracy score on a metasearch platform drops below a certain threshold, or if a reconciliation job detects a significant number of data discrepancies between the channel manager and an OTA."

            "You can see that the `Content-Type` header value is `application/x-www-form-urlencoded` , which does not match the OTA standard XML used in the request body. The correct `Content-Type` in this situation is `application/xml` or `text/xml` .",

            "The Booking.com Connectivity APIs accept requests in these formats:\n\n* **OTA** – OTA is an XML schema developed by the [OpenTravel Alliance](http://opentravel.org/) . It is tailored to the travel industry and provides online travel agencies and channel managers with a common data model to exchange information.\nYou can download the full OTA specification from the [OpenTravel Alliance website](http://opentravelmodel.net/pubs/specifications/Specifications.html) , or browse the data model [here](https://www.pilotfishtechnology.com/modelviewers/OTA/) . The Booking.com Connectivity APIs use OTA version 2003B. * **B.XML** – B.XML is an XML schema developed by Booking.com. OTA cannot support all the functionality we want to offer, so we use B.XML to fill in the gaps. * **JSON** \\- JSON stands for JavaScript Object Notation. Data is in name/value pairs, and is separated by commas. Curly braces hold objects and square brackets hold arrays. More fields and additional data will be added to the response structure as the API matures so it is advised for integrating clients to be receptive to these changes and not expect a strict schema. All request bodies must use UTF-8 encoding.",
            "* `application/xml` or `text/xml` : For the API endpoints that make use of OTA standard XML.",
            " * `application/json` : For the API endpoints that make use of JSON.",
